## Section 001: Rust Backend Runtime Operations
### Question 01: What decision does this reference help make?
- **current_section_observation:** Section 001 "Rust Backend Runtime Operations", Question 01 on governing decision: the seed currently shows that the seed title names runtime operations but the seed never states the ops file's organizing arc, its eight sections walk a service from boot to rollout, configuration parsed once at startup, secrets redacted, traces correlated, readiness distinguished from liveness, CI gated, images kept small, and schema changes sequenced for zero downtime
- **supporting_reason:** Section 001 "Rust Backend Runtime Operations", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision matters because operational failures cluster at transitions, boot, deploy, and migrate, so the file front-loads fail-fast behavior, invalid configuration should kill the process at startup rather than misbehave at runtime, and impossible rollout orderings should be rejected at planning rather than discovered in production
- **counterargument_or_limit:** Section 001 "Rust Backend Runtime Operations", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision must not conceal this limit: reading operations as polish applied after delivery inverts the file's stance, its own framing treats tracing, correlation, health checks, and failure classification as design concerns baked in from the start
- **assumptions_and_boundaries:** Section 001 "Rust Backend Runtime Operations", Question 01 on governing decision: apply the decision only within this boundary: this reference covers configuration, secrets, telemetry, CI, containers, and rollout for Rust backend services, service anatomy belongs to the playbook theme and auth or retry semantics to the security sibling
- **revision_decision:** Section 001 "Rust Backend Runtime Operations", Question 01 on governing decision: revise the section to open with the boot-to-rollout arc so readers see one lifecycle, not eight disconnected ops topics
- **additional_insight_to_add:** Section 001 "Rust Backend Runtime Operations", Question 01 on governing decision: add the consequence that six of the eight sections defend a single principle, the process must never run in a state it cannot explain, typed settings, redacted secrets, correlated traces, and honest readiness are all explanations the service carries about itself
### Question 02: What is the recommended default, and why?
- **current_section_observation:** Section 001 "Rust Backend Runtime Operations", Question 02 on recommended default: the defensible default is to parse configuration once at startup into typed settings, fail fast on invalid state, and plan every schema change as an ordered multi-step rollout
- **supporting_reason:** Section 001 "Rust Backend Runtime Operations", Question 02 on recommended default: prefer that default because operational failures cluster at transitions, boot, deploy, and migrate, so the file front-loads fail-fast behavior, invalid configuration should kill the process at startup rather than misbehave at runtime, and impossible rollout orderings should be rejected at planning rather than discovered in production
- **counterargument_or_limit:** Section 001 "Rust Backend Runtime Operations", Question 02 on recommended default: do not present the default as universal; reading operations as polish applied after delivery inverts the file's stance, its own framing treats tracing, correlation, health checks, and failure classification as design concerns baked in from the start
- **assumptions_and_boundaries:** Section 001 "Rust Backend Runtime Operations", Question 02 on recommended default: the default assumes the following scope: this reference covers configuration, secrets, telemetry, CI, containers, and rollout for Rust backend services, service anatomy belongs to the playbook theme and auth or retry semantics to the security sibling
- **revision_decision:** Section 001 "Rust Backend Runtime Operations", Question 02 on recommended default: make the default operational by stating both this action and its handoff: open with the boot-to-rollout arc so readers see one lifecycle, not eight disconnected ops topics
- **additional_insight_to_add:** Section 001 "Rust Backend Runtime Operations", Question 02 on recommended default: explain that the default also implies six of the eight sections defend a single principle, the process must never run in a state it cannot explain, typed settings, redacted secrets, correlated traces, and honest readiness are all explanations the service carries about itself

### Question 01: What decision does this reference help make?

- **supporting_reason:** Section 002 "Source Evidence Mapping Table", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision matters because operational guidance is chained guidance, a CI expectation references tests defined in the testing sibling and a rollout step references schema shapes owned by persistence doctrine in the playbook, so cross-file claims need by-name citations beyond the single mapped row
- **counterargument_or_limit:** Section 002 "Source Evidence Mapping Table", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision must not conceal this limit: attributing a chained claim to the mapped row alone, for instance quoting harness isolation rules as ops-file content, breaks the provenance the table exists to preserve
- **assumptions_and_boundaries:** Section 002 "Source Evidence Mapping Table", Question 01 on governing decision: apply the decision only within this boundary: provenance for this document's statements only, the table does not vouch for the four external URLs, none retrieved during this evolution
- **revision_decision:** Section 002 "Source Evidence Mapping Table", Question 01 on governing decision: revise the section to anchor on the ops file and cite chained bundle files explicitly wherever their content, not just their existence, is used

## Section 008: Verification Gate Command Set
### Question 01: What decision does this reference help make?
- **current_section_observation:** Section 008 "Verification Gate Command Set", Question 01 on governing decision: the seed currently shows that the seed document verifier stands alone while the ops file defines the delivery gate floor, formatting, linting, tests, and build at minimum, plus dependency hygiene where already enforced, and migration-aware tests in CI whenever database work is involved
- **supporting_reason:** Section 008 "Verification Gate Command Set", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision matters because the file scopes gates by change class, every change pays the four-gate floor, schema-touching changes additionally prove the application's expectations match the migrated schema, because green unit tests over a stale schema certify nothing
- **counterargument_or_limit:** Section 008 "Verification Gate Command Set", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision must not conceal this limit: a pipeline that skips migration-aware testing passes builds whose first production contact with the new schema is the deploy itself, converting CI's cheapest catch into rollout's most expensive one
- **assumptions_and_boundaries:** Section 008 "Verification Gate Command Set", Question 01 on governing decision: apply the decision only within this boundary: delivery-pipeline gates for this theme, runtime verification like health probes belongs to the observability section and endpoint verification to the testing sibling's matrix
- **revision_decision:** Section 008 "Verification Gate Command Set", Question 01 on governing decision: revise the section to adopt the four-gate floor with the conditional migration gate, and add the operational review questions, boot-failure behavior demonstrated and per-step rollback named
- **additional_insight_to_add:** Section 008 "Verification Gate Command Set", Question 01 on governing decision: add the consequence that the conditional structure is the design, unconditional heavyweight gates get disabled under deadline pressure, gating migration tests on migration presence keeps the pipeline cheap enough that nobody is tempted to bypass it

## Section 014: Worked Example Set
### Question 01: What decision does this reference help make?

- **supporting_reason:** Section 014 "Worked Example Set", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision matters because the four-step pattern is the file's most algorithmic content and its most catastrophic to improvise, a walkthrough that runs all four steps on a realistic table teaches the ordering logic that a rule statement cannot
- **counterargument_or_limit:** Section 014 "Worked Example Set", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision must not conceal this limit: engineers who have only read the pattern reliably compress it under pressure, merging expand and tighten into one migration because the data looks clean, the exact impossible ordering the walkthrough exists to make visceral

## Section 018: Workload Model
### Question 01: What decision does this reference help make?
- **current_section_observation:** Section 018 "Workload Model", Question 01 on governing decision: the seed currently shows that the seed endpoint-count budgets fit request work, not operational work, whose real units are per-service setup performed once, per-deploy checks performed constantly, and per-migration sequencing performed rarely at high stakes
- **supporting_reason:** Section 018 "Workload Model", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision matters because the three units scale on different axes, setup scales with service count, deploy checks scale with release frequency, and sequencing scales with schema churn, so a single capacity number cannot budget them together
- **counterargument_or_limit:** Section 018 "Workload Model", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision must not conceal this limit: budgeting operations as a flat tax breaks at migration time, a five-step sequenced change is a multi-day workload spike that flat models schedule as an afternoon, producing exactly the compressed impossible orderings the file forbids
- **assumptions_and_boundaries:** Section 018 "Workload Model", Question 01 on governing decision: apply the decision only within this boundary: budgeting operational effort for services adopting this theme, implementation-slice budgeting belongs to the playbook theme's model
- **revision_decision:** Section 018 "Workload Model", Question 01 on governing decision: revise the section to restate the model in three units, setup cost per new service, marginal check cost per deploy, and spike cost per sequenced migration with its five-delivery expansion made explicit

### Question 02: What is the recommended default, and why?
- **current_section_observation:** Section 018 "Workload Model", Question 02 on recommended default: the defensible default is to invest setup fully at service birth, keep per-deploy checks automated below human attention cost, and schedule migrations as the multi-delivery projects they are
- **supporting_reason:** Section 018 "Workload Model", Question 02 on recommended default: prefer that default because the three units scale on different axes, setup scales with service count, deploy checks scale with release frequency, and sequencing scales with schema churn, so a single capacity number cannot budget them together
- **counterargument_or_limit:** Section 018 "Workload Model", Question 02 on recommended default: do not present the default as universal; budgeting operations as a flat tax breaks at migration time, a five-step sequenced change is a multi-day workload spike that flat models schedule as an afternoon, producing exactly the compressed impossible orderings the file forbids
- **assumptions_and_boundaries:** Section 018 "Workload Model", Question 02 on recommended default: the default assumes the following scope: budgeting operational effort for services adopting this theme, implementation-slice budgeting belongs to the playbook theme's model
- **revision_decision:** Section 018 "Workload Model", Question 02 on recommended default: make the default operational by stating both this action and its handoff: restate the model in three units, setup cost per new service, marginal check cost per deploy, and spike cost per sequenced migration with its five-delivery expansion made explicit

## Section 019: Reliability Target
### Question 01: What decision does this reference help make?
- **current_section_observation:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 01 on governing decision: the seed currently shows that the seed document-property thresholds stand where operational targets should, zero secrets in logs or panics ever, zero deploys of unvalidated configuration, every request traceable by one identifier end to end, and every rollout step leaving a demonstrated rollback
- **supporting_reason:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision matters because each target is an ops-file rule stated as an invariant, redaction rules imply the zero-leak target, fail-fast boot implies the validated-config target, correlation rules imply the traceability target, and the sequencing questions imply the per-step rollback target
- **counterargument_or_limit:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision must not conceal this limit: targets averaged over time hide the absolutes that matter, a 99 percent secret-redaction rate is not near-success, one leaked key is a full rotation incident, so the first two targets are binary per event, not percentages
- **assumptions_and_boundaries:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 01 on governing decision: apply the decision only within this boundary: operational invariants for services under this theme, service-level latency targets belong to the performance section and correctness targets to the playbook theme
- **revision_decision:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 01 on governing decision: revise the section to publish the four targets with their evidence methods, log scanning for leaks, deploy-gate records for config validation, trace sampling for identifier propagation, and release records for rollback demonstrations
- **additional_insight_to_add:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 01 on governing decision: add the consequence that two targets are verifiable before production and two only in it, config validation and rollback demonstration are pre-deploy checkable, while leak absence and trace propagation need production sampling, so the target set spans both sides of the deploy boundary by construction
### Question 02: What is the recommended default, and why?
- **current_section_observation:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 02 on recommended default: the defensible default is to gate deploys on the two pre-deploy targets and sample production weekly for the two runtime ones
- **supporting_reason:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 02 on recommended default: prefer that default because each target is an ops-file rule stated as an invariant, redaction rules imply the zero-leak target, fail-fast boot implies the validated-config target, correlation rules imply the traceability target, and the sequencing questions imply the per-step rollback target
- **counterargument_or_limit:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 02 on recommended default: do not present the default as universal; targets averaged over time hide the absolutes that matter, a 99 percent secret-redaction rate is not near-success, one leaked key is a full rotation incident, so the first two targets are binary per event, not percentages
- **assumptions_and_boundaries:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 02 on recommended default: the default assumes the following scope: operational invariants for services under this theme, service-level latency targets belong to the performance section and correctness targets to the playbook theme
- **revision_decision:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 02 on recommended default: make the default operational by stating both this action and its handoff: publish the four targets with their evidence methods, log scanning for leaks, deploy-gate records for config validation, trace sampling for identifier propagation, and release records for rollback demonstrations
- **additional_insight_to_add:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 02 on recommended default: explain that the default also implies two targets are verifiable before production and two only in it, config validation and rollback demonstration are pre-deploy checkable, while leak absence and trace propagation need production sampling, so the target set spans both sides of the deploy boundary by construction

- **revision_decision:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 10 on second-order deductions: change the guidance by applying this concrete revision: publish the four targets with their evidence methods, log scanning for leaks, deploy-gate records for config validation, trace sampling for identifier propagation, and release records for rollback demonstrations
- **additional_insight_to_add:** Section 019 "Reliability Target", Question 10 on second-order deductions: the resulting operational insight is to change course when the default is unsafe or insufficient, using these bounded alternatives: chaos-style verification, deliberately injecting bad config and watching the boot die, heavier than record audits and the only direct test of fail-fast behavior under realistic conditions This changes how the section resolves which operational invariants a service must demonstrably hold for this theme's guidance to count as adopted.

## Section 021: Retry Backpressure Guidance
### Question 01: What decision does this reference help make?
- **current_section_observation:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 01 on governing decision: the seed currently shows that the seed corpus-process bullets stand where deploy-time retry doctrine should, failed deploys and failed migrations are retried events too, and their retry rules differ, a failed boot may redeploy freely, a failed migration step must never simply rerun
- **supporting_reason:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision matters because boot failures are idempotent by the fail-fast design, nothing happened, so retry is safe, while migration steps mutate durable state, so a mid-step failure leaves partial work whose rerun semantics depend on exactly which step and what it touched
- **counterargument_or_limit:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision must not conceal this limit: treating migration retry like deploy retry reruns a partially applied backfill or reapplies a constraint against half-moved data, converting a recoverable pause into the ambiguous state the sequencing questions exist to prevent
- **assumptions_and_boundaries:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 01 on governing decision: apply the decision only within this boundary: retry semantics for operational events, request-level retry and idempotency doctrine belongs to the security sibling's territory and is routed there
- **revision_decision:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 01 on governing decision: revise the section to add the two-regime rule, free retry for pre-traffic failures like boot and health, and stop-and-assess for any failure inside a sequenced migration, with the current step's rollback answer as the assessment's first input
- **additional_insight_to_add:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 01 on governing decision: add the consequence that the sequencing list pre-answers the assessment, because each step was planned with a what-rollback-still-works answer, a mid-step failure consults a decision already made calmly at planning time rather than improvising one at incident tempo, the ceremony's payoff is precisely this pre-computation
### Question 02: What is the recommended default, and why?
- **current_section_observation:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 02 on recommended default: the defensible default is to automate retries for boot-class failures with a crash-loop ceiling, and page a human with the step's planned rollback answer for migration-class failures
- **supporting_reason:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 02 on recommended default: prefer that default because boot failures are idempotent by the fail-fast design, nothing happened, so retry is safe, while migration steps mutate durable state, so a mid-step failure leaves partial work whose rerun semantics depend on exactly which step and what it touched

- **assumptions_and_boundaries:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 02 on recommended default: the default assumes the following scope: retry semantics for operational events, request-level retry and idempotency doctrine belongs to the security sibling's territory and is routed there
- **revision_decision:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 02 on recommended default: make the default operational by stating both this action and its handoff: add the two-regime rule, free retry for pre-traffic failures like boot and health, and stop-and-assess for any failure inside a sequenced migration, with the current step's rollback answer as the assessment's first input

### Question 10: What deeper deductions, second-order consequences, or additional insights follow, and how should they change the guidance?
- **current_section_observation:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 10 on second-order deductions: the deeper deduction is that the sequencing list pre-answers the assessment, because each step was planned with a what-rollback-still-works answer, a mid-step failure consults a decision already made calmly at planning time rather than improvising one at incident tempo, the ceremony's payoff is precisely this pre-computation
- **supporting_reason:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 10 on second-order deductions: that deduction follows from boot failures are idempotent by the fail-fast design, nothing happened, so retry is safe, while migration steps mutate durable state, so a mid-step failure leaves partial work whose rerun semantics depend on exactly which step and what it touched
- **counterargument_or_limit:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 10 on second-order deductions: its reach is limited where treating migration retry like deploy retry reruns a partially applied backfill or reapplies a constraint against half-moved data, converting a recoverable pause into the ambiguous state the sequencing questions exist to prevent
- **assumptions_and_boundaries:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 10 on second-order deductions: carry the deduction forward only within retry semantics for operational events, request-level retry and idempotency doctrine belongs to the security sibling's territory and is routed there
- **revision_decision:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 10 on second-order deductions: change the guidance by applying this concrete revision: add the two-regime rule, free retry for pre-traffic failures like boot and health, and stop-and-assess for any failure inside a sequenced migration, with the current step's rollback answer as the assessment's first input
- **additional_insight_to_add:** Section 021 "Retry Backpressure Guidance", Question 10 on second-order deductions: the resulting operational insight is to change course when the default is unsafe or insufficient, using these bounded alternatives: fully automated migration rollback on failure, attractive and dangerous, automated reversal of a partial backfill can destroy the evidence needed to fix forward, human assessment earns its latency here This changes how the section resolves which operational failures may retry automatically and which must stop for assessment.

## Section 022: Observability Checklist

## Section 023: Performance Verification Method
### Question 01: What decision does this reference help make?
- **current_section_observation:** Section 023 "Performance Verification Method", Question 01 on governing decision: the seed currently shows that the seed request-latency ceilings measure the wrong layer for this theme, whose performance surfaces are boot time to readiness, deploy pipeline duration, image build time under Rust's compilation economics, and telemetry overhead per request
- **supporting_reason:** Section 023 "Performance Verification Method", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision matters because the file touches each surface with a stated concern, fail-fast boots should die quickly and readiness should reflect truth promptly, CI gates bound pipeline time, the Docker section names build caching for Rust dependency compilation explicitly, and curated fields bound trace cost
- **counterargument_or_limit:** Section 023 "Performance Verification Method", Question 01 on governing decision: the decision must not conceal this limit: ignoring the operational surfaces lets them decay into delivery friction, a forty-minute uncached image build taxes every deploy, and slow readiness delays every rollout's traffic shift, costs that never appear in request percentiles
- **assumptions_and_boundaries:** Section 023 "Performance Verification Method", Question 01 on governing decision: apply the decision only within this boundary: verifying operational performance, request-path latency methods belong to the playbook theme and its SLO precondition
- **revision_decision:** Section 023 "Performance Verification Method", Question 01 on governing decision: revise the section to measure the four surfaces, boot-to-ready duration, pipeline wall time, image build time with and without warm cache, and traced-versus-untraced request overhead, each with a trend alarm rather than a fixed ceiling
- **additional_insight_to_add:** Section 023 "Performance Verification Method", Question 01 on governing decision: add the consequence that trend alarms fit operational surfaces better than ceilings because the surfaces degrade by accretion, one added dependency, one more gate, one extra recorded field, each individually reasonable, the alarm catches the slope that no single change trips
